Mecha is a term used in science fiction to describe large, piloted robots or machines, typically humanoid, controlled by a person from an internal cockpit. The word "mecha" comes from the Japanese term for "mechanical" (ãƒ¡ã‚«, meka), a shortening of the English loanword "mechanism" or "mechanical." In fiction, mecha are often depicted as powerful war machines, ranging from several meters to massive sizes, used in combat, construction, or other roles. They differ from powered armor (like Iron Man's suit) by being larger and typically piloted from a cockpit rather than worn. Origins and Key Figures: The mecha genre emerged in post-World War II Japan, influenced by rapid technological growth and cultural fascination with robotics. Mitsuteru Yokoyama created Tetsujin 28-go (1956, known as Gigantor in the West), one of the earliest mecha works, featuring a remotely controlled giant robot. Go Nagai revolutionized the genre with Mazinger Z (1972), introducing the concept of a giant robot piloted from inside, setting a standard for modern mecha. Yoshiyuki Tomino further defined the genre with Mobile Suit Gundam (1979), introducing the "real robot" subgenre, where mecha are treated as realistic military technology with complex narratives. Examples in Media: Anime/Manga: Mobile Suit Gundam, Neon Genesis Evangelion, Macross, Patlabor. Films: Pacific Rim (Jaegers), Avatar (Amplified Mobility Platforms), District 9 (Exo-suit). Video Games: MechWarrior, Armored Core, Super Robot Wars, Mecha BREAK. Subgenres: Super Robot: Features fantastical, superhero-like robots with quasi-magical abilities, often piloted by teenagers (e.g., Mazinger Z). Real Robot: Depicts mecha as realistic military tools, subject to physics and maintenance, with complex characters (e.g., Gundam).
